Early Arguments for Finitude of Past Events
Al-Razi presents the early arguments and the opponent's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early kalam arguments for why past events must be finite, including the superimposition proof, and the main objection raised against it.

Core Idea
If past events were infinite, contradictory conclusions would follow — so they must be finite.

Imam al-Razi begins by presenting the arguments of the early theologians (al-awā'il) for the finitude of past events. These arguments rely on the nature of events as originated, the applicability of increase and decrease, and a sophisticated superimposition argument. However, opponents raised a critical objection that the superimposition only occurs in estimation, not reality. This section sets the stage for al-Razi's own resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the early arguments
Read the first set of arguments: (1) each event originated so whole originated, (2) increase/decrease applies, (3) the superimposition argument. Note the objection that the whole may differ from parts.
The analogical argument about God's knowledge and power is also relevant.
2
Understand the superimposition argument
This is the central proof: by comparing two sequences of past events starting from different points, the necessity of finitude is established. Grasp the logical steps.
The argument requires careful attention to the idea of 'starting from now' vs 'starting from last year'.
3
Examine the opponent's objection
The opponent argues that the superimposition is only imaginary, not real, and the infinite cannot be imagined. Also, increase/decrease is only on the finite side.
This objection sets up the need for al-Razi's refined approach.
4
Reflect on the dialectical style
Notice how the passage is structured as a debate: argument, objection, and then later a response. This is typical of kalam literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأربع المذكورة وهو امتناع وجو حوادث لا أول لها في جانب الماضى فنورد أولا ماقيل فيه وعليه ثم أذكر ما عندى فيه فأقول. الأوائل قالوا في وجوب تناهى الحوادث الماضية: أنه لما كان كل واحد منها حادثا كان الكل حادثا. اعترض عليه بأن حكم الكل ربما مخالف الحكم على الآحاد. ثم قالوا الزيادة والنقصان بتطرة ان إلى الحوادث الماضية فتكون متناهية، وعرض معلومات الله تعالى ومقدوراته فإن الأولى أكثر من الثانية مع كونهما غير متناهين. ثم قال المحملون منهم الحوادث الماضية إذا أخذت تارة مبتدأة من الآن ذاهبة فى الماضى وتارة مبتدأة في مثل هذا الوقت من السنة الماضية ذاهبة في الماضى وأطبقت إحداهما على الأخرى فى النوهم، بأن يجعل المبدأ واحدا وهما في الذهاب إلى الماضى متطابقان استحال تساويهما، وإلا كان وجود الحوادث الواقعة في الزمان الذى هى الآن وهى السنة الماضية وعدمها واحدا، واستحال كون المبتدأة من السنة الماضية زائدة على المبتدأة من الآن لأن ما ينقص من المتساويين لا يكون زائدا على كل واحد منهما فإداً يجب أن يكون المبتدأة من السنة الماضية في جانب الماضى أنقص من المبتدأة الآن فى ذلك الجانب، ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بانتهائه قبل انتهاء المبتدأة من الآن ويكون الأنقص متناهيا والزائد عليه بمقدار متناه يكون متناعيا فيكون الكل متناهيا. واعتراض الخصم عليهم بأن هذا التطبيق لا يقع إلا فى الوهم وذلك يكون بشرط ارتسام المطابقين فيه وغير المتناهى لا يرتسم في الوهم، ومن البين أنهما لا يحصلان في الوجود مما فضلا عن توهم التطبيق فيهما في الوجود، فإذا هذا الدليل موقوف على حصول ما لا يحصل لا فى الوهم ولا فى الوجود وأيضاً الزيادة والنقصان إنما فرض في الطرف المتناهى لا فى الطرف الذى وقع النزاع فى تناديه فهو غير مؤثر فيه فهذا حاصل كلام، في هذا الموضع. وأنا أقول: إن كل حادث موصوف بكونه سابقا على ما بعده ويكون لاحقا بما قبله والاعتبار ان مختلفان، فإذا اعتبرنا الحوادث الماضية المبتدأة من الآن تارة من حيث كل واحد منها ساق وتارة من حيث هو بعينه لاحق كانت السوابق واللواحق المنباينين بالاعتبار متطابقين فى الوجود ولا يحتاج في تطابق هما إلى توهم تطبيق ومع ذلك يجب كون السوابق أكثر من اللواحق فى الجانب الذى وقع النزاع فيه، إذا اللواحق متناهية في الماضى لوجوب انقطاعها قبل انقطاع السوابق، والسوابق الزائدة عليها بمقدار متناه متناهية أيضاً، فإذا قد تم الدليل في سقوط ما اعترض عليه منه ويتم بذلك الدليل على حدوث العالم بطريقة الجمهور، هذا ما عندى فيه وأعود إلى النظر فيها في الكتاب.
The aforementioned four [issues], namely the impossibility of the existence of events without a beginning in the past. So we first present what was said for and against it, then I mention what I have regarding it, and I say: The earliest scholars said regarding the necessity of the finitude of past events: since each one of them is originated, the whole is originated. An objection was raised against them that the judgment of the whole may differ from the judgment of the individuals. Then they said: increase and decrease apply to past events, so they are finite. And the field of God's knowledge and His power: the former is greater than the latter despite both being infinite. Then the [proponents] among them said: past events if taken sometimes starting from now going into the past, and sometimes starting from the same time last year going into the past, and one is superimposed on the other in estimation, by making the beginning one and they are identical in going into the past, their equality is impossible, otherwise the existence of events occurring at the time that is now and last year and their non-existence would be one, and it is impossible for the starting point from last year to exceed the starting point from now because what is subtracted from two equals cannot be greater than each one. Therefore the starting point from last year must be less in the past direction than the starting point from now, and that is impossible unless it ends before the ending of the starting point from now, and the lesser is finite and the greater by a finite amount is finite, so the whole is finite. And the opponent's objection against them: this superimposition occurs only in estimation, and that requires the representation of the two superimposable objects in it, and the infinite is not represented in estimation, and it is obvious that they do not exist in reality, let alone the estimation of superimposition in existence. So this proof depends on the attainment of what is unattainable neither in estimation nor in existence. Also increase and decrease are assumed in the finite side, not in the side regarding which the dispute is about its finitude, so it is not effective in it. This is the gist of the discourse on this point. And I say: Every event is characterized by being prior to what comes after and posterior to what precedes it, and the two considerations are different. So if we consider past events starting from now, sometimes as each one being prior and sometimes as each one being posterior, the priors and posters differentiated by consideration are identical in existence, and their superimposition does not require estimation, yet the priors must be more than the posters in the direction under dispute, since the posters are finite in the past because they must end before the priors end, and the priors exceeding them by a finite amount are also finite. Thus the proof is complete in refuting the objection against it, and by that the proof for the temporality of the world is established according to the method of the majority. This is what I hold regarding it, and I return to the discussion in the book.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This long passage is a complete argument for the impossibility of an infinite regress of past events, leading to a proof that the world is temporally originated (hadith). It is structured as a dialectical exchange. First, the early scholars argue that since each past event is originated, the totality must be originated—but an objection notes that the whole may differ from its parts. Then they propose that increase and decrease apply to past events, so they must be finite; this is supported by an analogy with God's knowledge and power, where knowledge is greater yet both infinite, implying that past events are finite because they are subject to increase. The main proof involves superimposing two series of past events starting from different points, leading to a contradiction if they were infinite. The opponent objects that the superimposition is only imaginary and the infinite cannot be imagined; also that increase/decrease applies only to the finite side. Al-Razi then gives his own response: by considering each event as both prior and posterior (relative to others), the series of priors and posters are identical in existence but the priors must outnumber the posters in the past direction; since posters are finite (they must stop before priors), the priors—being finite in excess—also must be finite. Thus the regress is impossible, proving the world's temporal origination.

Key Terms
تناهى الحوادث
حوادث لا أول لها
التطبيق
الزيادة والنقصان

C) Increase and decrease do not apply to past events.
D) The two series are never actually equal.

Key Takeaway
The early theologians used the superimposition argument to show past events must be finite, but opponents objected that it relies on imagination, not reality.

?
Al-Razi records that the superimposition argument depends on estimation (wahm), not reality. In what area of your own beliefs might you be mistaking an imagined construct for a proven truth? Will you re-examine that belief today, even if it is uncomfortable?

Al-Razi's Refined Argument for Finitude
Al-Razi presents his own argument for finitude using priority and posteriority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's own response to the objection, which uses the concepts of priority and posteriority to prove the finitude of past events and the origination of the world.

Core Idea
By considering each event as both prior and posterior, the necessity of finitude becomes clear without relying on imagination.

After presenting the early arguments and their objection, Imam al-Razi provides his own solution. He observes that each event is both prior (to later events) and posterior (to earlier events). Considering these two aspects separately, the series of priors and posters are identical in existence but the priors must outnumber the posters in the past direction. Since the posters are finite, the priors—and hence all past events—must also be finite. This elegantly avoids the opponent's objection and establishes a sound proof for the world's temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual aspect of events
Al-Razi says every event is described as prior (sābiq) to what comes after and posterior (lāhiq) to what precedes. These are two different considerations (iʿtibārān).
This is the key insight.
2
Apply the two considerations to the series
Consider past events from now: if we take each event as prior, we get one series; if as posterior, another. These two series are the same events but considered from different angles.
They are identical in existence (mutābiqān fī l-wujūd).
3
Compare the numbers of priors and posters
Since each posterior must be preceded by a prior, the number of priors in the past must be greater than the number of posters. But the posters are finite because they must end before the priors end (since a posterior requires a prior before it). Therefore the priors are also finite, and thus all past events are finite.
The opponent's objection about imagination is irrelevant because this reasoning uses actual existence, not mental superimposition.
4
Conclude the proof for the world's temporality
Al-Razi states that this completes the proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) according to the method of the majority.
This is a major conclusion in kalam.

Source Text

The aforementioned four [issues], namely the impossibility of the existence of events without a beginning in the past. So we first present what was said for and against it, then I mention what I have regarding it, and I say: The earliest scholars said regarding the necessity of the finitude of past events: since each one of them is originated, the whole is originated. An objection was raised against them that the judgment of the whole may differ from the judgment of the individuals. Then they said: increase and decrease apply to past events, so they are finite. And the field of God's knowledge and His power: the former is greater than the latter despite both being infinite. Then the [proponents] among them said: past events if taken sometimes starting from now going into the past, and sometimes starting from the same time last year going into the past, and one is superimposed on the other in estimation, by making the beginning one and they are identical in going into the past, their equality is impossible, otherwise the existence of events occurring at the time that is now and last year and their non-existence would be one, and it is impossible for the starting point from last year to exceed the starting point from now because what is subtracted from two equals cannot be greater than each one. Therefore the starting point from last year must be less in the past direction than the starting point from now, and that is impossible unless it ends before the ending of the starting point from now, and the lesser is finite and the greater by a finite amount is finite, so the whole is finite. So this proof depends on the attainment of what is unattainable neither in estimation nor in existence. Also increase and decrease are assumed in the finite side, not in the side regarding which the dispute is about its finitude, so it is not effective in it. This is the gist of the discourse on this point. And I say: Every event is characterized by being prior to what comes after and posterior to what precedes it, and the two considerations are different. This is what I hold regarding it, and I return to the discussion in the book.

Commentary
This long passage is a complete argument for the impossibility of an infinite regress of past events, leading to a proof that the world is temporally originated (hadith). It is structured as a dialectical exchange. First, the early scholars argue that since each past event is originated, the totality must be originated—but an objection notes that the whole may differ from its parts. Then they propose that increase and decrease apply to past events, so they must be finite; this is supported by an analogy with God's knowledge and power, where knowledge is greater yet both infinite, implying that past events are finite because they are subject to increase. The main proof involves superimposing two series of past events starting from different points, leading to a contradiction if they were infinite. The opponent objects that the superimposition is only imaginary and the infinite cannot be imagined; also that increase/decrease applies only to the finite side. Al-Razi then gives his own response: by considering each event as both prior and posterior (relative to others), the series of priors and posters are identical in existence but the priors must outnumber the posters in the past direction; since posters are finite (they must stop before priors), the priors—being finite in excess—also must be finite. Thus the regress is impossible, proving the world's temporal origination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for finitude of past events depends on imagining infinite series and superimposing them in the mind.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the argument works by considering actual existence and the relations of priority and posteriority, without needing to imagine or superimpose series.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's argument, why does the series of priors outnumber the series of posters?
A) Because priors are infinite and posters are finite.
B) Because each event is prior to what follows and posterior to what precedes, and in the past direction there are more priors.

D) Because superimposition shows equality.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves the finitude of past events by comparing the series of priors and posters, showing that the priors must exceed the posters by a finite amount, making the whole finite and establishing the world's origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life as a series of events—each one dependent on what came before—do you feel the weight of your own finitude? How does that realization push you to orient every moment toward the One who is not an event?
?
Al-Razi argues that every event is both prior and posterior, yet you are not just a link in a chain. You are a conscious being who can reflect on this dependency. If your existence is borrowed from each prior moment, what have you done today to acknowledge the One who holds your existence from moment to moment?
?
This proof shows that the world could not have an infinite past—it had a beginning. If your own story began only recently, and you will return to your Maker, what are you doing now to ensure that your finite moments are spent in gratitude and obedience, rather than forgetfulness?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Razi's Argument and the Philosophers' Proofs
Razi states his own argument and enumerates three philosophical proofs for the world's eternity without immediate refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's central argument that the world is not necessarily eternal because what was not eternal in the past remains receptive to non-existence, and you will be able to identify the three key philosophical proofs for the world's eternity that he sets out to refute.

Core Idea
If the world was not eternal in the past, its essence is always receptive to non-existence.

Razi begins by presenting his own concise argument against the world's necessary eternity, then lays out the three main proofs the philosophers use to argue for its eternity. Though his refutations are not included here, understanding these proofs is essential for appreciating the full debate.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's own argument
Start by reading the opening lines where Razi states his position: whatever was not eternal in the past (azalī) cannot be eternal in the future (abadī) because its essence is inherently receptive to non-existence, a permanent concomitant.
Notice the causal link: 'lack of azalī' implies 'receptivity to non-existence' implies 'not abadī'.
2
Examine the first philosophical proof: God as Necessitating Cause
The philosophers argue that since God (the efficient cause) is a necessitating cause by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), His own eternity necessitates the eternity of the world.
This is the famous 'emanation' argument: if cause is eternal, effect must be eternal.
3
Examine the second proof: Impossibility of Time's Annihilation
They argue that if time were to cease after existing, its non-existence would be after its existence in time, creating a contradiction: time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent.
This is a logical puzzle about temporal asymmetry.
4
Examine the third proof: Necessity of a Substrate (Hayula)
Everything that can cease to exist requires a substrate (hayula) to sustain the possibility of its non-existence. If the world could cease, it would need an eternal matter that never ceases.
This leads to the conclusion that matter (hayula) is eternal and never without form, so body cannot become non-existent.

Source Text

أحدهما أن حدوث الضد يتوقف على انتفاء الضد الآخر فلو كان انتفاء الضد الآخر معللا بحدوث هذا الضد لزم الدور وهو محال أن التضاد حاصل فى الجانبين وليس انتفاء أحدهما أولى من العكس فإما أن ينتفى كل واحد A question: The world is not necessarily eternal, contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya. For we [argue]: that which was not eternal must not be eternal, because whatever was not eternal, its essence was receptive to non-existence, and that receptivity is not [merely] concomitant with that essence? Rather, it is from the necessary concomitants of that essence, so the essence is always receptive to non-existence. As for the philosophers, they adduced several [arguments]: first, that the efficient cause of the world is necessitating by essence (mujib bi al-dhat), so the permanence of [the cause] entails the permanence of the world. Second, that if time were annihilated, its non-existence after its existence would be posterior in time, so time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent, which is a contradiction. Third, that everything that admits non-existence, its possibility of non-existence is present before its non-existence, and that possibility must have a locus (mahall), i.e., there must be something that is described by that non-existence, and that is not the existence of the thing because that which can be described by something must be established with that thing, and the existence of that thing is its non-existence. So there must be another thing that sustains the possibility of its non-existence, and that is the matter (hayula). Thus everything that admits non-existence has a matter; if non-existence were admitted for the matter, it would require another matter ad infinitum. Therefore matter does not admit non-existence, and it has been established that matter is never free of corporeal form, so the non-existence of body is impossible. The Karramiyya Muslims argued for the necessity of the world's eternity [as follows]: the non-existence of the world after its existence must be either by the annihilating act of an annihilator, or by the advent of an opposite, or by the removal of a condition. All three divisions are invalid, so the claim of the world's non-existence after its existence is invalid. As for [the first], we say that it is not permissible for it to be annihilated by an annihilation act because if the annihilation is an existent thing, then that existence is not identical with the non-existence of the world, otherwise existence would be identical with non-existence. Rather, its utmost is the substance? That would be annihilation by opposite, and this is not this division but the second. And if it is not existent, it is pure non-existence, so it is impossible to attribute it to an agent, because reason makes no distinction between saying 'He did nothing at all' and saying 'He produced non-existence.' Otherwise, one non-existence would differ from another, so each non-existence would have change and stability, so non-existence would be something positive, which is a contradiction. And as for [the second], we say it is not permissible for it to be annihilated by the advent of an opposite for two reasons. First: the advent of the opposite depends on the removal of the other opposite, so if the removal of the other opposite were caused by the advent of this opposite, a circularity would arise, which is impossible. [Second]: opposition exists on both sides, and the removal of one is no more likely than the reversal. So either each of them is removed by the other, which is impossible because the efficient cause of the non-existence of each is the existence of the other, and the efficient cause is simultaneous with the effect, so if both effects occurred together, both existences would occur together, thus they would be existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is impossible. Or neither is removed by the other, so the two opposites would coexist. It cannot be said: the newly originated is stronger than the remaining because the newly originated at the moment of its origination is attached to [the eternal?] and the remaining is not so, and because the newly originated...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a new mas'ala (question) in which Razi asserts that the world is not necessarily eternal (abadī), contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya. He first gives his own argument: whatever is not eternal in the past (azalī) must also not be eternal in the future, because its essence is inherently receptive to non-existence, and this receptivity is a permanent concomitant of that essence. Then he refutes three proofs from the philosophers: (1) if God is a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat), His eternity would entail the world's eternity; (2) the impossibility of time's annihilation because its non-existence would be after its existence, requiring time to exist at the moment of assumed non-existence; (3) the need for a substrate (hayula) to sustain the possibility of non-existence, leading to the conclusion that matter is eternal and never lacking form, so body cannot cease. After this, Razi addresses the Karramiyya—a sect that apparently affirmed the world's eternity. They argued that the world's non-existence after existence could only occur via an external agent's act, a contrary's advent, or removal of a condition; all three being impossible. Razi refutes each: (a) an annihilating act cannot be existent (because then it would be identical with what it destroys) or non-existent (because pure non-existence cannot be attributed to an agent); (b) the advent of a contrary leads to circularity or simultaneous existence of opposites; (c) the removal of a condition is also problematic. The passage ends with an objection ('It cannot be said...') that is cut off, likely continuing the refutation. The overall structure is: Razi states his position, presents the opponents' arguments, and then refutes them systematically.

Key Terms
azali
abadi
mujib bi al-dhat
hayula

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's own argument, what is the relationship between something not being eternal in the past (azalī) and its being eternal in the future (abadī)?
A) If something is not azalī, it must be abadī.
B) If something is not azalī, it cannot be abadī.
C) If something is azalī, it cannot be abadī.
D) There is no relationship between azalī and abadī.

Key Takeaway
Razi's own argument is that receptivity to non-existence is a permanent concomitant of an essence that was not eternal, so it cannot be eternal in the future; the philosophers' three proofs relate to God's nature, time, and matter, and must be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every created thing is, by its very essence, always receptive to non-existence, then what truly anchors your sense of security? Are you building your life on things that could vanish at any moment, or on the One who alone is self-subsistent?
?
The philosophers argued that God's nature forces Him to create eternally. Do you ever treat your relationship with God as a mechanical necessity—expecting blessings based on your actions—rather than a living, sovereign relationship where He acts by will?
?
Razi states that the world is not necessarily eternal, meaning it had a beginning and could have an end. If you truly believed that this world is fleeting and your own existence is contingent, what would you do differently starting today?

Refuting the Karramiyya's Argument for the World's Eternity
Razi refutes the Karramiyya's argument by dismantling their threefold division of possible causes of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya's threefold argument for the world's necessary eternity and Razi's systematic refutation of each division, including the logical problems of annihilation by act, advent of opposite, and removal of condition.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya's exhaustive argument is dismantled by showing each option leads to absurdity.

After presenting the philosophers' proofs, Razi turns to the Karramiyya, who argued that the world's non-existence after existence is impossible based on an exhaustive division of possible causes. Razi refutes each division in detail, revealing logical contradictions and circularities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Karramiyya's three divisions
The Karramiyya claim that the world's non-existence after existence can only occur via: (a) an act of an external annihilator, (b) the advent of an opposite, or (c) the removal of a condition. They argue all three are impossible.
This is a classic trilemma often used by mutakallimun.
2
Examine Razi's refutation of the first division (annihilation by act)
Razi argues that if the annihilating act is an existing thing, it cannot be identical with the non-existence it produces; if it is non-existent, it cannot be attributed to an agent because pure non-existence cannot be an act.
He also points out that if the act were existent, it would be an opposite, falling into the second division.
3
Examine Razi's refutation of the second division (advent of opposite)
Razi gives two reasons: (1) the advent of an opposite depends on the removal of the other opposite, leading to circularity (dawr); (2) opposition is symmetrical, so either each opposite removes the other (impossible) or neither does (coexistence of opposites).
He also rejects the objection that the newly originated is 'stronger' than the remaining.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a new mas'ala (question) in which Razi asserts that the world is not necessarily eternal (abadī), contrary to the philosophers and the Karramiyya. Key Terms
isnad
taqabul

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Karramiyya's exhaustive division is logically sound and covers all possibilities for the world's non-existence.
Correction: Razi shows that the first division collapses into the second and that the second leads to circularity or simultaneous opposites, so the division is not genuinely exhaustive or coherent.

Checkpoint
In refuting the second division (advent of opposite), what is Razi's first reason?
A) The advent of an opposite depends on the removal of the other opposite, leading to circularity.
B) The advent of an opposite is impossible because it would require time to exist.
C) The advent of an opposite is impossible because it would require a necessary being.
D) The advent of an opposite is not a real cause.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's claim that the world must be eternal because none of the three possible causes of non-existence can occur is refuted by showing that each supposed cause either self-destructs or reduces to another problematic division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you cannot conceive of a thing, it cannot be? How does this passage expose the limitations of human logic when applied to divine action? What change in your attitude towards God's power is required?
?
The Karramiyya attempted to prove the world eternal using reason. Where in your own life have you tried to confine God's decrees within your own logical expectations? How can you actively submit to God's absolute freedom to create and annihilate as He wills?
?
Razi argues that non-existence cannot be caused by an efficient cause. Do you ever treat God's non-action as if it were a kind of action? How might this affect your understanding of divine wisdom in allowing things to cease? What does it mean to trust that God's withholding of existence is not a 'doing' but a wise refrain?

Philosophers' Proof: Celestial Bodies are Simple, Spherical, and Moved by Will
Philosophers' argument for the simplicity and spherical shape of celestial bodies and their voluntary motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that celestial bodies are simple, spherical, and move by a voluntary will, not by nature or force.

Core Idea
Celestial bodies move voluntarily because they are neither driven by nature nor forced.

The philosophers present an intricate argument to establish the nature of the heavenly spheres. They begin by showing that direction exists and is indivisible, leading to a spherical determiner that defines above and below. From its properties, they deduce that its motion cannot be natural or forced, hence it must be voluntary. This passage reveals the rational framework behind their view of the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the passage from 'أما الأجسام الفلكية' to the end of the argument. Notice how each claim builds on the previous one.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Understand direction (al-jiha)
See why the philosophers claim direction must exist and be indivisible. Follow the reasoning about the mover and the limit.
3
Follow the spherical determiner
From the existence of direction, they deduce a spherical determiner (muhaddad kuri) that defines above and below by its center and circumference.
4
Grasp simplicity and motion
Understand why the determiner is simple (not composed) and why it cannot have rectilinear motion, leading to its circular motion.
5
Analyze the nature of motion
Consider why the motion cannot be natural or forced, leading to the conclusion that it is voluntary, making the heaven a living being.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأجسام الفلكية فقد زعمت الفلاسفة أنها لا ثقيلة ولا خفيفة ولا حارة ولا باردة ولا رطبة ولا يابسة ، ولا يصح الخرق والالتئام والكون والفساد عليها واحتجوا بأن الجهة مقصد المتحرك ومتعلق الإشارة فتكون موجودة لأن النفى لا تميز فيه وهى غير منقسمة وإلا لكان المتحرك إذا وصل إلى أحد تصفيها وبقى متحركا فإما أن يقال إنه لا يتحرك عن الجهة فتكون الجهة ذلك الحد لاما ورائه أو إليها فحينئذ لا يكون ذلك الحد من الجهة بل الجهة ما وراؤه فثبت أن الجهة حد غير منقسم ثم بينوا أنه لا بد من محدد كرى يتحدد الفوق والتحت بمحيطه ومركزه ثم قالوا وهذا المحدد غير قابل للحركة المستقيمة وإلا كانت الجهتان أعنى ما عنه وما إليه خاصلتين لا به وإذا لم يكن قابلا للحركة المستقيمة لزم أن لا يكون ثقيلا ولا خفيفا لأن الثقيل هو الذي يصعد عنه وذلك حركة مستقيمة ولم يقبل الخرق والالتئام لأن ذلك حركة مستقيمة وإذا لم يقبل الخرق كان بسيطا لأن كل مركب قابل للانحلال وكل بسيط فكل جزء يفرض فيه يمكن أن يتحصل على الوضع الذى حصل عليه الجزء الآخر وكل ما كان كذلك كان قابلا للحركة وكل ما كان كذلك ففيه ميل يحركه وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متحرك بالاستدارة فحركته ليست بطبيعية وإلا لتحرك بالطبع عما عنه يحرك بالطبع فيكون الطبيعة الواحدة طالبة للشيء الواحد وهاربه عنه وهو محال ولا قسرية لأن القسر ما يكون على خلاف الطبع وهناك لا طبع فلا قسر فتلك الحركة إرادية فالسماء حيوان متحرك بالإرادة والجواب عن هذه الحكايات مستقصى فى كتاب الكلامية والحكمية
(1) أقول : إنما بنى الفلاسفة إثبات الجهات ومحددوها على القول بتناهى الابعاد ، وقالوا لما كانت الابعاد متناهية فالإشارة الحسية لا يمكن أن تذهب إلى غير النهاية ولا المتحرك القاصد جهة ولوجوب كون المشار إليه بالحس موجودا تكون الجهة موجودة وكل موجود قابل للإشارة ، فإما أن يكون جسما أو جسمانيا ولا يجوز أن تكون الجهة جسما لأن كل جسم قابل للتجزئة ولا شيء من الجهة بقابل لها لما ذكره ، فإذا الجهة جسمانية غير قابلة للقسمة ، وكل جهة تشتمل على مأخذين بضرورة ، والجسم الذى تتحدد به الجهة لا يجوز أن يركب من أجزاء مختلفة لكونها مختلفة الجهات ووجوب كون الجهات متقدمة على أجزائها ثم عليها ، فإذا المحدد يكون بسيطا في نفسه متشابها في شكله ولا يتشابه فى الشكل غير الكرة فإذا ه و كرة ، ولا يمكن أن يحدد هو خارج عنه لاحتياجه فى التعلق بما هو خارج عنه إلى الجهة المتقدمة عليها فإذا تحدد ما هو داخل فيه ولا تميز فيما هو داخل فيه باعتبار الجهة إلا بالمركز أو بالمحيط فاذا يتحدد به جهتان هما مأخذا امتداد واحد لا غير وهما العلو والسفل ، وما عداهما لا يكون متميزا بالطبع بل إن كان فيها امتياز كان بالعرض كاليمين والشمال وبساطة المحدد يمكن أن يبين بما قلنا وإن كان بيانها بامتناع الفرق عليه أيضا ممكن كما ذكره وأما بيان وجوب الحركة فى المحدد فلا يتأتى إلا بمقدمتين إحداهما أن الجسم لا يخلو عن ميل وثانيهما أن الجسم البسيط يمتنع أن يكون فيه ميلان مختلفى الجهة ويمتنع أن يتحرك المحدد حركة غير المستديرة فإذا فيها ميل مستدر
As for celestial bodies, the philosophers claimed that they are neither heavy nor light, neither hot nor cold, neither wet nor dry, and that rupture and reunion, generation and corruption do not occur in them. They argued that direction (al-jiha) is the intended goal of a mover and the object of indication, so it must exist because negation has no distinction, and it is indivisible; otherwise if a mover reached one of its halves and remained moving, it would be said either that it does not move from the direction, so the direction is that limit and not beyond it, or to it, so then that limit is not part of the direction, but the direction is what is beyond it. Thus it is established that the direction is an indivisible limit. Then they showed that there must be a spherical determiner (muhaddad kuri) by which the above and below are determined by its circumference and center. Then they said: This determiner is not susceptible to rectilinear motion, otherwise both directions—namely, from which and to which—would subsist in two [places], not in it. Since it is not susceptible to rectilinear motion, it follows that it is neither heavy nor light, because the heavy is that which moves away from it, and that is rectilinear motion; nor does it admit rupture and reunion because that is rectilinear motion. Since it does not admit rupture, it is simple, because every composite is capable of dissolution, and every simple, each part supposed in it can obtain the position that another part obtained, and everything like that is capable of motion, and everything like that has an inclination (mayl) that moves it, and everything like that moves circularly. So its motion is not natural, otherwise it would naturally move from what it naturally moves from, so one nature would seek one thing and flee from it, which is impossible; nor is it forced, because force is contrary to nature, and there is no nature here, so no force. So that motion is voluntary, so the heaven is a living being moving by will. The answer to these accounts is detailed in the book of kalam and philosophy.
(1) I say: The philosophers founded the proof of directions and their determiners on the assertion of the finitude of dimensions. They said: Since dimensions are finite, sensory indication cannot go to infinity, nor can a mover intending a direction. Because what is indicated by sense must exist, the direction exists. Every existent is subject to indication, so it is either a body or a bodily (jismani). It is not permissible that the direction be a body, because every body is divisible, and nothing of the direction is divisible for the reason mentioned. So the direction is a bodily indivisible. Every direction necessarily comprises two extremities. The body by which the direction is determined cannot be composed of different parts because of their different directions and the necessity that directions be prior to its parts and then prior to it. So the determiner is simple in itself, similar in its shape; nothing resembles it in shape except the sphere, so it is a sphere. It cannot be determined by something outside it because its relation to what is outside would require a direction prior to it. So it determines what is inside it, and there is no distinction in what is inside it by way of direction except by the center or the circumference. So two directions are determined by it, which are the two extremities of one extension only: the above and the below. What is other than them is not distinguished by nature; if there is distinction, it is accidental like right and left. The simplicity of the determiner can be shown by what we said, though its demonstration by the impossibility of [؟] is also possible as he mentioned. As for the demonstration of the necessity of motion in the determiner, it is not achieved except through two premises: one, that a body is not free from inclination; two, that a simple body cannot have two inclinations in different directions, and it is impossible for the determiner to move other than circularly. So it has a circular inclination.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the nature of celestial bodies and then provides a commentary expanding on the reasoning. The philosophers claim celestial bodies lack the four primary qualities and are not subject to generation and corruption. Their proof begins by establishing that direction (al-jiha) exists and is indivisible. They argue that since motion requires a direction, and sensible pointing must point to something existent, direction must exist. It cannot be divisible because if it were, a mover could stop at one half and still be moving, leading to absurdities. Thus direction is an indivisible limit. From this, they infer there must be a spherical determiner (muhaddad kuri) that defines the above and below by its center and circumference. This determiner cannot undergo rectilinear motion because that would imply two directions (from which and to which) not inherent in it. Therefore it is neither heavy nor light, and not subject to rupture. Since it doesn't admit rupture, it is simple. Every simple body with parts that can exchange positions is capable of motion and possesses an inclination (mayl) leading to circular motion. This motion cannot be natural (because natural motion would imply one nature seeking and fleeing the same thing) nor forced (since force requires a contrary nature, and there is no nature here). Hence the motion is voluntary, making the heaven a living being moving by will. The author then refers to a fuller answer elsewhere. The commentary (1) elaborates on the philosophical foundation, explaining that the proof depends on the finitude of dimensions, and details how direction is a bodily indivisible, the determiner must be spherical, and concludes with two premises for circular motion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial motion is natural or forced like earthly motion.
Correction: The philosophers argue celestial bodies are not subject to natural motion because they have no natural inclination (heavy/light) and no contrary nature, so motion must be voluntary.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers claim the motion of the heaven is voluntary?
A) Because it is heavy and moves downward
B) Because it is neither natural nor forced
C) Because it is forced by an external agent
D) Because it has a nature that seeks the center

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that celestial bodies are simple, spherical, and move by will because their motion cannot be attributed to nature or force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argued that one nature cannot both seek and flee the same thing. Where in your own life do you find a contradiction—being drawn to Allah while also fleeing from His commands? How does this passage expose that inconsistency, and what step will you take today to resolve it?
?
If the heavens move by will, not by force or blind nature, then every rotation of the planets is an act of obedience. When you look up at the sky tonight, will you remember that you are part of a creation that obeys its Lord willingly? What does that demand from you in your next action?
?
The passage says the heaven is a 'living being moving by will' (al-samā' ḥayawān mutaḥarrik bil-irāda). If the vastness of the cosmos lives and moves by its Creator's permission, how does that change your sense of your own smallness—and your responsibility to align your will with His? What is the one area where your will rebels, and what will you do about it before this day ends?

Elaboration of the Philosophers' Proof: Foundations of Direction and Spherical Determiner
Author's commentary explaining the foundational premises of the philosophers' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational premises underlying the philosophers' proof, particularly the finitude of dimensions and the nature of direction as a bodily indivisible, and the two premises for circular motion.

Core Idea
The philosophers' argument rests on the finitude of dimensions and the indivisibility of direction.

In his commentary, the author unpacks the logical foundation of the philosophers' proof. He explains that the existence of direction depends on the finitude of dimensions, and shows that direction is a bodily indivisible. He then derives the spherical shape of the determiner and finally presents two key premises that necessitate circular motion. This elaboration clarifies the rational structure of the earlier argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the passage beginning with 'I say' and note how it rephrases and expands the earlier argument.
This passage is a commentary on the philosophers' proof.
2
Understand the foundation: finitude of dimensions
The author states that the entire proof is built on the assertion that dimensions are finite. This allows sensory indication and motion to have a limit.
3
Grasp direction as bodily indivisible
Follow the reasoning that direction exists, is not a body (since bodies are divisible), but is a bodily (jismani) indivisible. It comprises two extremities (above and below).
4
Derive the spherical determiner
The determiner must be simple and spherical because it cannot be composed of different parts (due to differing directions) and cannot be determined by something outside itself.
5
Learn the two premises for motion
The author presents two premises: (1) every body has an inclination; (2) a simple body cannot have two inclinations in opposite directions. Hence the determiner must have a single circular inclination.

(1) أقول : إنما بنى الفلاسفة إثبات الجهات ومحددوها على القول بتناهى الابعاد ، وقالوا لما كانت الابعاد متناهية فالإشارة الحسية لا يمكن أن تذهب إلى غير النهاية ولا المتحرك القاصد جهة ولوجوب كون المشار إليه بالحس موجودا تكون الجهة موجودة وكل موجود قابل للإشارة ، فإما أن يكون جسما أو جسمانيا ولا يجوز أن تكون الجهة جسما لأن كل جسم قابل للتجزئة ولا شيء من الجهة بقابل لها لما ذكره ، فإذا الجهة جسمانية غير قابلة للقسمة ، وكل جهة تشتمل على مأخذين بضرورة ، والجسم الذى تتحدد به الجهة لا يجوز أن يركب من أجزاء مختلفة لكونها مختلفة الجهات ووجوب كون الجهات متقدمة على أجزائها ثم عليها ، فإذا المحدد يكون بسيطا في نفسه متشابها في شكله ولا يتشابه فى الشكل غير الكرة فإذا ه و كرة ، ولا يمكن أن يحدد هو خارج عنه لاحتياجه فى التعلق بما هو خارج عنه إلى الجهة المتقدمة عليها فإذا تحدد ما هو داخل فيه ولا تميز فيما هو داخل فيه باعتبار الجهة إلا بالمركز أو بالمحيط فاذا يتحدد به جهتان هما مأخذا امتداد واحد لا غير وهما العلو والسفل ، وما عداهما لا يكون متميزا بالطبع بل إن كان فيها امتياز كان بالعرض كاليمين والشمال وبساطة المحدد يمكن أن يبين بما قلنا وإن كان بيانها بامتناع الفرق عليه أيضا ممكن كما ذكره وأما بيان وجوب الحركة فى المحدد فلا يتأتى إلا بمقدمتين إحداهما أن الجسم لا يخلو عن ميل وثانيهما أن الجسم البسيط يمتنع أن يكون فيه ميلان مختلفى الجهة ويمتنع أن يتحرك المحدد حركة غير المستديرة فإذا فيها ميل مستدر

?

What is the 'inclination' of your heart right now, and does it align with the circular motion of the angels around the Throne? Commit today to a specific act that redirects your motion toward Allah.

Proof from the Origination of Bodies
Imam Razi begins the section on divine matters and presents the first proof of God's existence from bodies' origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the existence of God based on the origination of bodies, as presented by Imam Razi, including its key premises and the role of possibility.

Core Idea
The proof from origination uses possibility to link an originated world to a Creator.

This lesson introduces the first of four arguments for God's existence that Imam Razi examines. Beginning from the fact that the world is composed of bodies, the argument proceeds through the origination (huduth) of these bodies to the necessity of a creator. However, the reasoning requires an intermediate step: showing that an originated thing is possible, and that every possible thing needs an efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Imam Razi structures the argument.
The passage includes an editorial note from Razi himself.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof has two premises: (1) the world is originated, and (2) every originated thing has an originator. Locate these in the text.
3
Understand the proof of the second premise
Razi proves the second premise by showing that an originated thing is possible (capable of existence and non-existence) and that every possible thing requires an efficient cause.
The key link is the concept of possibility (imkan).
4
Consider the editorial note
Read Razi's remark about later theologians who argue that the need for an originator is not self-evident from origination alone but must be proved via possibility.
This shows the refinement of the argument.
5
Reflect on the broader context
Think about why this argument is attributed to Prophet Abraham and how it fits into the cosmological argument tradition.
The phrase 'I do not love the setting ones' is a reference to Quran 6:76.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الثالث في الإلهيات والنظر في الذات والصفات والأفعال والأسماء
( القسم الأول في الذات )
قد عرفت أن العالم إما جواهر وإما أعراض ، وقد يستدل بكل واحد منهما على وجود الصانع إما بإمكانه أو حدوثه فهذه وجوه أربعة .
الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الأجسام وهو طريقة الخليل عليه السلام فى قوله : لا أحب الأفلين ، وتحريره أن العالم محدث وكل محدث فله محدث الأول تقدم وأما الثاني فالدليل عليه أن المحدث ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر ، أما أن المحدث ممكن فلان المحدث هو الذى كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً وما هذا شأنه كانت ماهيته قابلة العدم والوجود ولا معنى للممكن إلا هذا ، وأما إن الممكن لا بد له من مؤثر فقد تقدم
(۳) أقول : المتأخرون من المتكلمين يقولون : الحكم بأن كل محدث فلا بد له محدث من محتاج إلى الاستدلال بإمكانه على احتياجه إلى المحدث .
The third pillar on divine matters: consideration of the Essence, attributes, acts, and names. (First section on the Essence) You have known that the world is either substances or accidents. Each of them may be used to prove the existence of the Maker, either through its possibility or its origination; thus there are four aspects. The first: the proof by the origination of bodies, and it is the method of the Friend (Abraham), peace be upon him, in his statement: 'I do not love the setting ones.' Its formulation is that the world is originated, and every originated thing has an originator. The first premise is already established. As for the second, the proof for it is that the originated is possible, and every possible thing has an efficient cause. That the originated is possible is because the originated is that which was non-existent then became existent; whatever is such, its essence is capable of non-existence and existence, and there is no meaning for 'possible' except this. As for the fact that the possible must have an efficient cause, it has already preceded. (3) I say: The later theologians say that the judgment that every originated thing must have an originator is in need of proof by its possibility for its need of an originator.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the third pillar (الركن الثالث) of the book, devoted to divine matters (الإلهيات). It introduces the first section on the Essence (الذات). Imam Razi first reminds that the world consists of substances and accidents, and each can be used to prove the existence of the Creator in two ways: through possibility (imkan) or origination (huduth), yielding four arguments. He then presents the first argument: proof from the origination of bodies, citing Abraham's statement 'I do not love the setting ones' (Qur'an 6:76). The argument is formulated as: the world is originated; everything originated has an originator. The first premise (world's origination) is assumed established. The second premise is proven by showing that the originated is possible (because it was non-existent then existent, so its essence admits both existence and non-existence), and every possible thing requires an efficient cause (already proven earlier). The editorial note observes that later theologians argue that the claim 'every originated thing has an originator' itself requires proof through possibility—i.e., the need for an originator is not self-evident from origination alone but must be demonstrated via the possibility of the originated.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that origination directly proves a creator without any intermediate step.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the proof requires the intermediate concept of possibility: an originated thing is possible, and every possible thing needs an originator. The editorial note emphasizes this point.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof for the existence of the Creator presented by Imam Razi in this section?
A) Proof from the possibility of accidents.
B) Proof from the origination of bodies, attributed to Abraham.
C) Proof from the composition of substances.
D) Proof from the necessity of a cause for every effect.

Key Takeaway
The first proof of God's existence from the origination of bodies relies on the intermediate concept of possibility: an originated thing is possible, and every possible requires a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just understood that everything originated is possible and depends on a cause. Look at your own existence: you were once non-existent. Can you genuinely accept that your very being is a gift sustained moment by moment by the Creator? How will you live today as someone who is radically dependent on Allah, not on your own strength or plans?
?
Abraham, peace be upon him, said, 'I do not love the setting ones.' What are the 'setting' things in your life—people, status, wealth, or ambitions that fade? Have you placed your ultimate trust in anything that will inevitably 'set'? How can you use Abraham's example to realign your heart's attachment to the One who never sets?
?
The proof from possibility demands that you admit: if you are possible, you need a cause. But do you live as though you are self-sufficient? When you feel proud of your achievements, do you remember that even your ability to act is created? What concrete steps can you take to embed the reality of your contingency into your daily habits, so that pride is replaced by gratitude?

Introduction to Divine Essence and the Four Proofs
Razi introduces the divine section and enumerates the four methods of proving the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the third pillar of al-Muhassal and the four main methods of proving the existence of the Maker through the world.

Core Idea
The world's substances and accidents each provide two paths to the Creator: possibility and temporality.

Imam Razi opens the divine section by outlining the topics: Essence, attributes, actions, and names. He then summarizes the logical routes from the world to its Creator. The world is either substances or accidents, and each can be used to prove the existence of the Maker either through its inherent possibility (imkan) or its temporal origination (huduth). This yields four distinct proofs, the first of which is the famous argument from createdness of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the third pillar
Identify the four topics: Essence, attributes, actions, names. Note that the first section is on the Essence.
This sets the scope for the entire divine section.
2
Understand the classification of proofs
Razi states that the world consists of substances and accidents, and each can be used to demonstrate the Maker through possibility or temporality. Count the four combinations.
This shows the systematic approach of kalām.
3
Identify the first method
The first method is inference from the temporality of bodies, which is the method of Abraham (peace be upon him).
Abraham's argument is a key proof in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الثالث في الإلهيات والنظر في الذات والصفات والأفعال والأسماء. القسم الأول في الذات. قد عرفت أن العالم إما جواهر وإما أعراض، وقد يستدل بكل واحد منهما على وجود الصانع إما بإمكانه أو حدوثه فهذه وجوه أربعة. الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الأجسام وهو طريقة الخليل عليه السلام في قوله : لا أحب الأفلين، وتحريره أن العالم محدث وكل محدث فله محدث الأول تقدم وأما الثاني فالدليل عليه أن المحدث ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر، أما أن المحدث ممكن فلان المحدث هو الذي كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً وما هذا شأنه كانت ماهيته قابلة العدم والوجود ولا معنى للممكن إلا هذا، وأما إن الممكن لا بد له من مؤثر فقد تقدم.
The third pillar: On divine matters, dealing with the Essence, attributes, actions, and names. The first section: On the Essence. You have known that the world consists of either substances or accidents. Each of them may be used to demonstrate the existence of the Maker, either through its possibility or its temporality (creation). Thus, there are four aspects. The first: Inference from the temporality (creation) of bodies. This is the method of al-Khalil (Abraham), peace be upon him, in his statement: 'I do not love those that set.' Its analysis: The world is temporal (created), and every temporal being has a creator, the first [premise] has been established; as for the second, the proof is that the temporal being is possible, and every possible being has an efficient cause. That the temporal being is possible is because it is that which was non-existent then came into existence, and such a thing's essence is receptive to non-existence and existence, and there is no meaning of 'possible' except this. As for that the possible must have an efficient cause, it has already been discussed.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage opens the third pillar of the book, on divinity (al-ilahiyyat). It distinguishes sections on Essence, attributes, actions, and names. In section one on the Essence, the author summarizes that the world is substances and accidents, and each can be used to prove the Maker either through its possibility (imkan) or its temporality (huduth), giving four methods. The first method is inference from the temporality (creation) of bodies, exemplified by Abraham's statement in the Quran: 'I do not love those that set' (Surah al-An'am, 76). The full reasoning: The world is temporal; every temporal has a creator (first premise already proven). The second premise: the temporal is possible (because it was non-existent then existed, thus receptive to both). And every possible needs an efficient cause (already proven earlier). Thus the proof is complete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that every substance and accident around you can serve as a proof for the Creator. Look at your own hand: it is a substance, and its movements are accidents. If you had to prove God's existence to a skeptic using only your hand, which of the four proofs would you employ? Craft your argument in one sentence, and say it out loud. If you cannot, then you have not truly owned this knowledge. Commit to practicing one of these proofs before you sleep tonight.
?
Razi breaks the world into substances and accidents. In your daily life, do you tend to focus more on the substances (people, objects) or the accidents (feelings, thoughts, circumstances)? How has this imbalance affected your connection to the Creator? Today, make a conscious effort to see both categories as signs, and write down one substance and one accident you will reflect on tomorrow morning as a proof of God.
?
This lesson tells you there are four ways to prove the Creator, but knowledge without action is empty. When was the last time you actually used one of these arguments to strengthen your certainty? If it has been a while, what is the real reason—laziness, distraction, or a lack of conviction that these proofs matter? Pause and ask yourself: if you truly believed that these arguments are keys to unwavering faith, would you treat them with such neglect? Make a firm intention to recite one proof each day this week.

The Proof from Creation: Abraham's Argument
Razi details the logical steps of Abraham's proof from the temporality of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Abraham's argument for the existence of God based on the temporality of bodies.

Core Idea
The temporal nature of the world shows it is contingent and therefore requires a creator.

This passage presents the first of the four proofs: the inference from the createdness of bodies. Razi attributes this to Prophet Abraham, who said 'I do not love those that set.' The proof is structured as a syllogism: the world is temporal, every temporal being has a creator, and this is established by showing that the temporal is possible and every possible requires an efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The proof has two premises: (1) the world is temporal, (2) every temporal has a creator. Razi indicates the first is already established, and focuses on proving the second.
The second premise is the heart of the proof.
2
Understand why the temporal is possible
A temporal being is one that was non-existent and then came to exist; its essence is receptive to both existence and non-existence, which is the definition of the possible (contingent).
This links temporality to contingency.
3
Recall the principle of efficient cause
Razi refers to an earlier discussion that every possible needs an efficient cause (mu'aththir).
This principle was established in the previous sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الثالث في الإلهيات والنظر في الذات والصفات والأفعال والأسماء. القسم الأول في الذات. قد عرفت أن العالم إما جواهر وإما أعراض، وقد يستدل بكل واحد منهما على وجود الصانع إما بإمكانه أو حدوثه فهذه وجوه أربعة. الأول : الاستدلال بحدوث الأجسام وهو طريقة الخليل عليه السلام في قوله : لا أحب الأفلين، وتحريره أن العالم محدث وكل محدث فله محدث الأول تقدم وأما الثاني فالدليل عليه أن المحدث ممكن وكل ممكن فله مؤثر، أما أن المحدث ممكن فلان المحدث هو الذي كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً وما هذا شأنه كانت ماهيته قابلة العدم والوجود ولا معنى للممكن إلا هذا، وأما إن الممكن لا بد له من مؤثر فقد تقدم.
The third pillar: On divine matters, dealing with the Essence, attributes, actions, and names. The first section: On the Essence. You have known that the world consists of either substances or accidents. Each of them may be used to demonstrate the existence of the Maker, either through its possibility or its temporality (creation). Thus, there are four aspects. The first: Inference from the temporality (creation) of bodies. This is the method of al-Khalil (Abraham), peace be upon him, in his statement: 'I do not love those that set.' Its analysis: The world is temporal (created), and every temporal being has a creator, the first [premise] has been established; as for the second, the proof is that the temporal being is possible, and every possible being has an efficient cause. That the temporal being is possible is because it is that which was non-existent then came into existence, and such a thing's essence is receptive to non-existence and existence, and there is no meaning of 'possible' except this. As for that the possible must have an efficient cause, it has already been discussed.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage opens the third pillar of the book, on divinity (al-ilahiyyat). It distinguishes sections on Essence, attributes, actions, and names. In section one on the Essence, the author summarizes that the world is substances and accidents, and each can be used to prove the Maker either through its possibility (imkan) or its temporality (huduth), giving four methods. The first method is inference from the temporality (creation) of bodies, exemplified by Abraham's statement in the Quran: 'I do not love those that set' (Surah al-An'am, 76). The full reasoning: The world is temporal; every temporal has a creator (first premise already proven). The second premise: the temporal is possible (because it was non-existent then existed, thus receptive to both). And every possible needs an efficient cause (already proven earlier). ?

?

Do you merely accept the proof intellectually, or does it move you to the same kind of radical devotion that Abraham had? What would it take for that proof to become a lived reality rather than a theological fact?

The Nature of the Possible: Objection and Answer
Razi presents and refutes an objection that a temporal being could be necessary in itself after creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the objection that a temporal being could be necessary in itself after creation.

Core Idea
Possibility is an intrinsic property of the essence, not a function of time.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi defends the standard Ash'ari position that every temporal being is possible (contingent). He addresses a sophisticated objection: if a thing was non-existent then exists, why can't it be considered necessary of existence at the time of its existence? Razi's answer clarifies the meaning of possibility (al-qubul) and shows that the essence's indifference to existence and non-existence is timeless.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Begin by reading the passage from 'فإن قيل' to the first 'قلنا'. Notice that the opponent claims the discussion is settled except for the premise that every temporal being is possible.
2
Identify the opponent's argument about essence changing modality
The opponent argues that the possible could have been necessary of nonexistence before creation and necessary of existence after. trace the reasoning: if something has a beginning, before that it was impossible, then possible, so why not necessary?
3
Examine Razi's definition of 'possible'
Razi clarifies that 'possible' does not mean the essence is established in a state of existence or non-existence, but rather that the intellect does not prevent it from remaining or ceasing.
This is the core of his response; possibility is intrinsic, not temporal.
4
Understand Razi's rejection of temporal variation
Razi concedes that impossibility and necessity depend on specific times, but the essence considered apart from time retains only receptivity (al-qubul).
5
Note the final rebuttal about the first moment
The opponent's final point about the first moment of existence is refuted: if there were a first moment, a moment earlier would entail eternity, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الكلام على هذه المقدمات قد تقدم إلا على قولنا إن كل محدث ممكن. قوله: المحدث كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً فيكون قابلاً للعدم والوجود لا محالة. قلنا: من مذهبكم أن المعدوم ليس بشيء لا عين ولا ذات بل كان نفياً محضاً، وإذا كان كذلك استحال الحكم عليه بالقبول، سلمنا صحة الحكم عليه، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنها حين كانت معدومة كانت واجبة العدم لعينها ثم في زمان وجودها صارت واجبة الوجود لعينها، تقريره أن الشيء بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم لا يجوز في العقل فرض تقدمه لا إلى أول وإلا لزم صحة كون الشيء مع كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم أزلياً وذلك محال، فإذا لصحة وجوده بداية فقبل تلك الصحة كان ممتنعاً لذاته ثم انقلب ممكناً لذاته، وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال كان ممتنعاً لذاته ثم انقلب واجباً لذاته. والجواب: أن قولنا الممكن قابل للوجود والعدم لا نعني به أن تلك الماهية متقررة حالة الوجود والعدم بل نعني به أن الماهية لا يمتنع في العقل بقاؤها كما كانت ولا يمتنع في العقل بطلانها. قوله: لم لا يجوز أن تكون الماهية ممتنعة لذاتها في وقت ثم تنقلب واجبة لذاتها في وقت آخر. قلنا: هب أن الأمر كذلك لكن حصول الامتناع يتوقف على حضور وقته المخصوص وحصول الوجوب يتوقف على حضور الوقت الآخر، والماهية من حيث هي هي مع قطع النظر عن الوقتين لا يبقى لها إلا القبول. قوله: الممكن المأخوذ بشرط كونه مسبوقاً بالعدم لصحة وجوده أول. قلنا: لا نسلم وإلا لزم أن يكون فرض دخوله في الوجود قبل ذلك الوقت بلحظة يوجب صيرورته أزلياً وذلك محال بالبديهة.
If it is said: The discussion on these premises has already preceded, except for our statement that every temporal being is possible. He said: The temporal being was non-existent then came into existence, so it is necessarily receptive to non-existence and existence. We say: According to your madhhab, the non-existent is not a thing, neither an entity nor an essence, but is pure negation. If so, it is impossible to judge it as having receptivity. We grant the validity of the judgment, but why may it not be said that when it was non-existent, it was necessary of non-existence for its own sake, then at the time of its existence it becomes necessary of existence for its own sake? The elaboration: A thing conditioned by being preceded by non-existence cannot be supposed in the intellect to have priority without a beginning, otherwise it would entail that a thing, despite being preceded by non-existence, is eternal, which is impossible. So since its existence has a beginning, before that beginning it was impossible in itself, then it turned into possible in itself. If that is allowed, why may it not be said that it was impossible in itself then turned into necessary in itself? The answer: Our statement 'the possible is receptive to existence and non-existence' does not mean that the essence is established in the state of existence or non-existence; rather we mean that the essence is not, in the intellect, prevented from remaining as it was, nor is it prevented from ceasing. He said: Why may the essence not be impossible in itself at one time and then become necessary in itself at another time? We say: Suppose that is so, but the attainment of impossibility depends on the presence of its specific time, and the attainment of necessity depends on the presence of the other time, and the essence considered in itself, disregarding the two times, has only receptivity left. He said: The possible taken with the condition of being preceded by non-existence: does its existence have a first? We say: We do not grant it; otherwise, supposing its entry into existence a moment before that time would entail its becoming eternal, and that is self-evidently impossible.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer sequence. The opponent objects that our claim that every temporal being is possible is not yet justified. Then we respond by analyzing the nature of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and the possible (al-mumkin). The objector raises a sophisticated argument: if a thing was non-existent then exists, perhaps it was necessarily non-existent in itself before, and then necessarily existent in itself after. The author answers by clarifying that 'possibility' refers to the essence's intrinsic indifference to existence/non-existence, not to a state. Further objections about the possibility of change in the essence's modal status are answered by pointing to the external factor of time: the essence itself, abstracted from time, has only receptivity. Another objection about the first moment of existence is rebutted by noting that if we allow a first moment, then a moment earlier would entail eternity, which is absurd. The author thus defends the standard Ash'ari position that possibility is an intrinsic property of the essence.

Key Terms
الممكن
المعدوم
الوجوب الذاتي
الامتناع الذاتي
القبول
البداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A temporal being could be necessary of existence for its own sake at the time of its existence.
Correction: Possibility (al-qubul) is an intrinsic property of the essence, not dependent on time. The essence itself, considered apart from time, is indifferent to existence and non-existence.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that a temporal being could be necessary of existence for its own sake at the time of its existence?
A) By denying that the thing ever exists.
B) By asserting that possibility is an intrinsic property of the essence apart from time.
C) By citing consensus of the scholars.
D) By admitting the objection but calling it irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Possibility (al-qubul) is the essence's indifference to existence and non-existence, unaffected by time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your own essence, considered in itself, is merely possible (mumkin). How does that truth confront the pride you may feel in your achievements? What would it mean to live each day conscious that you are not necessary in yourself?
?
If possibility is intrinsic and unchangeable, then your reliance on Allah is equally constant. Does your daily life reflect that constant need, or do you sometimes act as if you have become self-sufficient? What habit could you start today to remind yourself of your essential contingency?
?
The lesson argues that a thing cannot become necessary in itself after being possible. How does this affect your understanding of Allah's uniqueness as the only Necessary Being? Does this make your worship more focused, or does it feel distant? Why?

Allah's Existence

The second proof: establishing a single necessary being through contingency
Imam Razi outlines the proof from contingency and its two steps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second proof from contingency and why it first establishes the uniqueness of the Necessary Being before applying it to the world.

Core Idea
The proof from contingency must first establish that the Necessary Being cannot be more than one.

Imam Razi now presents the second argument — the proof from contingency. This proof does not start directly with the world; it first establishes a key principle: the Necessary Being must be unique. Only then does it look at contingent things to draw the conclusion. This passage outlines that sequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two-part structure. Note that it begins with establishing a principle before observing the world.
The phrase 'على ماهر' may be a scribal corruption; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the first claim about the Necessary Being
The passage states: 'he establishes the proof that the Necessary of Existence is impossible to be more than one.' This is the foundational step to avoid polytheism.
3
Identify the observation of contingent things
Then he observes in bodies a multiplicity of contingent things and asserts that every contingent has an influencer. This observation leads to the conclusion of a single necessary being.
The influencer is implied to be the Necessary Being already proven unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الثاني : الاستدلال بالإمكان وتقريره أن يقيم الدلالة على أن واجب الوجود يستحيل أن يكون أكثر من واحد ، ثم يشاهد فى الأجسام كثرة ممكنة وكل ممكن فله مؤثر على ماهر
The second way: the deduction from contingency, and its exposition is that he establishes the proof that the Necessary of Existence is impossible to be more than one, then he observes in bodies a multiplicity of contingent things, and every contingent has an influencer according to an expert.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage introduces the second proof (النظر الثاني) for the existence of God, which is the proof from contingency (الإمكان). Imam Razi states its method: first, one establishes that the Necessary of Existence (واجب الوجود) cannot be more than one. Then, one observes in the physical world (الأجسام) a large number of contingent things (كثرة ممكنة). The principle is that every contingent thing has an influencer (مؤثر). The text ends abruptly with 'على ماهر' which likely refers to a previously mentioned condition that the influencer must be wise or capable; this may be a scribal error for 'على ما مر' (as previously passed) or 'على ما تقدم' (as before). The proof essentially argues from the contingency of observed bodies to the necessity of a single necessary being that is their cause.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالإمكان
واجب الوجود
الممكن
المؤثر
الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The contingency proof alone can lead to multiple necessary beings.
Correction: The proof first establishes that the Necessary Being must be one before observing contingent things, thus precluding polytheism.

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the second proof (النظر الثاني) according to Imam Razi?
A) Proving that the world has a beginning.
B) Proving that the Necessary of Existence cannot be more than one.
C) Showing that bodies are composed of atoms.
D) Denying the existence of accidental attributes.

Key Takeaway
The proof from contingency begins by proving that the Necessary Being must be one, then uses the observation of contingent beings to infer its existence as their cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that every contingent thing has an influencer. Look at your own daily dependencies—your health, wealth, relationships. Do you honestly treat them as contingent, or do you cling to them as if they are self-sufficient? What would change if you deeply felt that only the One Necessary Being is truly independent? Commit to one concrete action today that reminds you of your utter reliance on Him.
?
The proof begins by ruling out more than one necessary being. But examine your heart: are there ‘gods’ you serve in your thoughts and time—such as status, comfort, or ambition—that you treat as ultimate? How would your day look if you consciously demoted them to mere contingent things? Write down one such false ‘necessary’ you need to dethrone.
?
The influencer in the proof is described as ‘expert’ (ماهر). This means the cause of your existence is not blind force but perfect wisdom. When you face trials or confusion, do you truly believe that the One who brought you into existence does so with precise knowledge? What is one area of your life where you need to trust His wisdom more than your own understanding?
